#aaa solid">NASA <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Flagship_Program" title="Flagship Program">Flagship Program</a></div><div style="width:100%"><div class="noprint" style="float: left; text-align:left;padding-right:0.5em;">&#8592;&#160;<a href="/wiki/Voyager_program" title="Voyager program"><i>Voyager</i> program</a></div><div class="noprint" style="float: right; text-align:right;padding-left:0.5em;"><i><a href="/wiki/Cassini%E2%80%93Huygens" title="Cassini&#8211;Huygens">Cassini&#8211;Huygens</a></i>&#160;&#8594;</div></div>&#160;</td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p><i><b>Galileo</b></i> was an American <a href="/wiki/Unmanned_spacecraft" title="Unmanned spacecraft">unmanned spacecraft</a> that studied the planet <a href="/wiki/Jupiter" title="Jupiter">Jupiter</a> and <a href="/wiki/Moons_of_Jupiter" title="Moons of Jupiter">its moons</a>, as well as several other <a href="/wiki/Solar_System" title="Solar System">Solar System</a> bodies. Named after the Italian astronomer <a href="/wiki/Galileo_Galilei" title="Galileo Galilei">Galileo Galilei</a>, it consisted of an orbiter and an entry probe. It was delivered into Earth orbit on October 18, 1989 by <span class="nowrap"><a href="/wiki/Space_Shuttle" title="Space Shuttle">Space Shuttle</a></span> <a href="/wiki/Space_Shuttle_Atlantis" title="Space Shuttle Atlantis"><i>Atlantis</i></a>. <i>Galileo</i> arrived at Jupiter on December 7, 1995, after <a href="/wiki/Gravity_assist" title="Gravity assist">gravitational assist</a> flybys of <a href="/wiki/Venus" title="Venus">Venus</a> and <a href="/wiki/Earth" title="Earth">Earth</a>, and became the first spacecraft to orbit Jupiter. It launched the first probe into Jupiter, directly measuring its <a href="/wiki/Atmosphere" title="Atmosphere">atmosphere</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-endkit_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-endkit-5">[5]</a></sup> Despite suffering major antenna problems, <i>Galileo</i> achieved the first <a href="/wiki/Asteroid" title="Asteroid">asteroid</a> flyby, of <a href="/wiki/951_Gaspra" title="951 Gaspra">951 Gaspra</a>, and discovered the first <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Asteroid_moon" title="Asteroid moon">asteroid moon</a>, Dactyl, around <a href="/wiki/243_Ida" title="243 Ida">243 Ida</a>. In 1994, <i>Galileo</i> observed <a href="/wiki/Comet_Shoemaker%E2%80%93Levy_9" title="Comet Shoemaker&#8211;Levy 9">Comet Shoemaker&#8211;Levy 9</a>\'s collision with Jupiter.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-endkit_5-1"><a href="#cite_note-endkit-5">[5]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Jupiter\'s atmospheric composition and <a href="/wiki/Ammonia" title="Ammonia">ammonia</a> clouds were recorded, the clouds possibly created by outflows from the lower depths of the atmosphere. <a href="/wiki/Io_(moon)" title="Io (moon)">Io</a>\'s <a href="/wiki/Volcanism" title="Volcanism">volcanism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Plasma_(physics)" title="Plasma (physics)">plasma</a> interactions with Jupiter\'s atmosphere were also recorded. The data <i>Galileo</i> collected supported <a href="/wiki/Europa_(moon)#Subsurface_ocean" title="Europa (moon)">the theory of a liquid ocean</a> under the icy surface of <a href="/wiki/Europa_(moon)" title="Europa (moon)">Europa</a>, and there were indications of similar liquid-<a href="/wiki/Saline_water" title="Saline water">saltwater</a> layers under the surfaces of <a href="/wiki/Ganymede_(moon)" title="Ganymede (moon)">Ganymede</a> and <a href="/wiki/Callisto_(moon)" title="Callisto (moon)">Callisto</a>. Ganymede was shown to possess a <a href="/wiki/Magnetic_field" title="Magnetic field">magnetic field</a> and the spacecraft found new evidence for <a href="/wiki/Exosphere" title="Exosphere">exospheres</a> around Europa, Ganymede, and Callisto.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-endkit_5-2"><a href="#cite_note-endkit-5">[5]</a></sup> <i>Galileo</i> also discovered that Jupiter\'s faint <a href="/wiki/Ring_system" title="Ring system">ring system</a> consists of dust from impacts on the four small inner moons. The extent and structure of Jupiter\'s <a href="/wiki/Magnetosphere" title="Magnetosphere">magnetosphere</a> was also mapped.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-endkit_5-3"><a href="#cite_note-endkit-5">[5]</a></sup>\n</p><p>On September 21, 2003, after 14 years in space and 8 years in the Jovian system, <i>Galileo</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">\'s</span> mission was terminated by sending it into Jupiter\'s atmosphere at a speed of over 48 kilometers per second (30&#160;mi/s), eliminating the possibility of <a href="/wiki/Interplanetary_contamination" title="Interplanetary contamination">contaminating local moons</a> with terrestrial bacteria.\n</p>\n<div class="toc" id="toc"><input class="toctogglecheckbox" id="toctogglecheckbox" role="button" style="display:none" type="checkbox"/><div class="toctitle" dir="ltr" lang="en"><h2>Contents</h2><span class="toctogglespan"><label class="tocnumber">11</span> <span class="toctext">Gallery of Jupiter system images</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-44"><a href="#See_also"><span class="tocnumber">12</span> <span class="toctext">See also</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-45"><a href="#References"><span class="tocnumber">13</span> <span class="toctext">References</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-46"><a href="#External_links"><span class="tocnumber">14</span> <span class="toctext">External links</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</div>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Background">Background</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Galileo_(spacecraft)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Background">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>Jupiter was rated as the number one priority in the <a href="/wiki/Planetary_Science_Decadal_Survey" title="Planetary Science Decadal Survey">Planetary Science Decadal Survey</a> published in the summer of 1968.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ssb1968_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-ssb1968-6">[6]</a></sup> In the early 1970s the first flybys of Jupiter were achieved by <i><a href="/wiki/Pioneer_10" title="Pioneer 10">Pioneer 10</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Pioneer_11" title="Pioneer 11">Pioneer 11</a></i>, and before the decade was out it was also visited by the more advanced <i><a href="/wiki/Voyager_1" title="Voyager 1">Voyager 1</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Voyager_2" title="Voyager 2">Voyager 2</a></i> spacecraft.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Mission_overview">Mission overview</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Galileo_(spacecraft)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Mission overview">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<table class="box-Refimprove_section plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content ambox-Refimprove" role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-image"><div style="width:52px"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Question_book-new.svg"><img alt="" data-file-height="399" data-file-width="512" decoding="async" height="39" .tmulti>.thumbinner{width:100%!important;max-width:none!important}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .tsingle{float:none!important;max-width:none!important;width:100%!important;text-align:center}}</style><div class="thumb tmulti tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:292px;max-width:292px"><div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:144px;max-width:144px"><div class="thumbimage" style="height:151px;overflow:hidden"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:STS-34_Launch_1.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="1432" data-file-width="1340" decoding="async" height="152" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/STS-34_Launch_1.jpg/142px-STS-34_Launch_1.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/STS-34_Launch_1.jpg/213px-STS-34_Launch_1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/STS-34_Launch_1.jpg/284px-STS-34_Launch_1.jpg 2x" width="142"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">Launch of <a href="/wiki/STS-34" title="STS-34">STS-34</a></div></div><div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:144px;max-width:144px"><div class="thumbimage" style="height:151px;overflow:hidden"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Galileo_Deployment_(high_res).jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="1000" data-file-width="940" decoding="async" height="151" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/eb/Galileo_Deployment_%28high_res%29.jpg/142px-Galileo_Deployment_%28high_res%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/eb/Galileo_Deployment_%28high_res%29.jpg/213px-Galileo_Deployment_%28high_res%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/eb/Galileo_Deployment_%28high_res%29.jpg/284px-Galileo_Deployment_%28high_res%29.jpg 2x" width="142"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left"><i>Galileo</i> is prepared for release from <span class="nowrap"><a href="/wiki/Space_Shuttle" title="Space Shuttle">Space Shuttle</a></span> <a href="/wiki/Space_Shuttle_Atlantis" title="Space Shuttle Atlantis"><i>Atlantis</i></a>. The <a href="/wiki/Inertial_Upper_Stage" title="Inertial Upper Stage">Inertial Upper Stage</a> (white) is attached.</div></div><div style="clear:left"></div></div></div>\n<p>Work on the spacecraft began at Jet Propulsion Laboratory in 1977, while the <i><a href="/wiki/Voyager_1" title="Voyager 1">Voyager 1</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Voyager_2" title="Voyager 2">2</a></i> missions were still being prepared for launch. Early plans called for a launch on <span class="nowrap"><a href="/wiki/Space_Shuttle" title="Space Shuttle">Space Shuttle</a></span> <a href="/wiki/Space_Shuttle_Columbia" title="Space Shuttle Columbia"><i>Columbia</i></a> on what was then codenamed <a href="/wiki/Canceled_Space_Shuttle_missions#STS-23_(Columbia)" title="Canceled Space Shuttle missions">STS-23</a> in January 1982, but delays in the development of the Space Shuttle allowed more time for development of the probe. As the shuttle program got underway, <i>Galileo</i> was scheduled for launch in 1984, but this later slipped to 1985 and then to 1986.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-tom_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-tom-7">[7]</a></sup> The mission was initially called the <i>Jupiter Orbiter Probe</i>; it was christened <i>Galileo</i> in 1978.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-explore_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-explore-8">[8]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Once the spacecraft was complete, its launch was scheduled for <a href="/wiki/Canceled_Space_Shuttle_missions#STS-61-G_(Atlantis)" title="Canceled Space Shuttle missions">STS-61-G</a> on-board  <a href="/wiki/Space_Shuttle_Atlantis" title="Space Shuttle Atlantis"><i>Atlantis</i></a> in 1986. The <a href="/wiki/Inertial_Upper_Stage" title="Inertial Upper Stage">Inertial Upper Stage</a> booster was going to be used at first, but this changed to the Centaur booster, then back to IUS after <i>Challenger</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-tom_7-1"><a href="#cite_note-tom-7">[7]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Centaur_(rocket_stage)" title="Centaur (rocket stage)">Centaur-G</a> liquid hydrogen-fueled booster stage allowed a direct trajectory to Jupiter. The mission was further delayed by the hiatus in launches that occurred after the <a href="/wiki/Space_Shuttle_Challenger_disaster" title="Space Shuttle Challenger disaster">Space Shuttle <i>Challenger</i> disaster</a>. New safety protocols introduced as a result of the disaster prohibited the use of the Centaur-G stage on the Shuttle, forcing <i>Galileo</i> to use a lower-powered <a href="/wiki/Inertial_Upper_Stage" title="Inertial Upper Stage">Inertial Upper Stage</a> solid-fuel booster. The mission was re-profiled in 1987 to use several <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gravitational_slingshot" title="Gravitational slingshot">gravitational slingshots</a>, referred to as the Venus-Earth-Earth Gravity Assist or VEEGA maneuvers, to provide the additional velocity required to reach its destination. It was finally launched on October 18, 1989, by <span class="nowrap"><a href="/wiki/Space_Shuttle" title="Space Shuttle">Space Shuttle</a></span> <a href="/wiki/Space_Shuttle_Atlantis" title="Space Shuttle Atlantis"><i>Atlantis</i></a> on the <a href="/wiki/STS-34" title="STS-34">STS-34</a> mission.\n</p>\n<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r856303569" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="thumb tmulti tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:492px;max-width:492px"><div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:244px;max-width:244px"><div class="thumbimage" style="height:181px;overflow:hidden"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Animation_of_Galileo_trajectory.gif"><img alt="" data-file-height="420" data-file-width="560" decoding="async" height="182" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/05/Animation_of_Galileo_trajectory.gif/242px-Animation_of_Galileo_trajectory.gif" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/05/Animation_of_Galileo_trajectory.gif/363px-Animation_of_Galileo_trajectory.gif 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/05/Animation_of_Galileo_trajectory.gif/484px-Animation_of_Galileo_trajectory.gif 2x" width="242"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">Animation of <i>Galileo</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">\'s</span> trajectory from October 19, 1989, to September 30, 2003 <br/> <span style="margin:0; font-size:90%; white-space:nowrap;"><span class="legend-text" style="border:none; padding:1px .6em; background-color:magenta; color:black; font-size:95%; line-height:1.25; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;<i>Galileo</i></span>&#160;<b>&#183;</b> <span style="margin:0; font-size:90%; white-space:nowrap;"><span class="legend-text" style="border:none; padding:1px .6em; background-color:lime; color:black; font-size:95%; line-height:1.25; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;<a href="/wiki/Jupiter" title="Jupiter">Jupiter</a></span>&#160;<b>&#183;</b> <span style="margin:0; font-size:90%; white-space:nowrap;"><span class="legend-text" style="border:none; padding:1px .6em; background-color:royalblue; color:black; font-size:95%; line-height:1.25; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;<a href="/wiki/Earth" title="Earth">Earth</a></span>&#160;<b>&#183;</b> <span style="margin:0; font-size:90%; white-space:nowrap;"><span class="legend-text" style="border:none; padding:1px .6em; background-color:PaleGreen; color:black; font-size:95%; line-height:1.25; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;<a href="/wiki/Venus" title="Venus">Venus</a></span>&#160;<b>&#183;</b> <span style="margin:0; font-size:90%; white-space:nowrap;"><span class="legend-text" style="border:none; padding:1px .6em; background-color:Gold; color:black; font-size:95%; line-height:1.25; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;<a href="/wiki/951_Gaspra" title="951 Gaspra">951 Gaspra</a></span>&#160;<b>&#183;</b> <span style="margin:0; font-size:90%; white-space:nowrap;"><span class="legend-text" style="border:none; padding:1px .6em; background-color:Cyan; color:black; font-size:95%; line-height:1.25; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;<a href="/wiki/243_Ida" title="243 Ida">243 Ida</a></span></div></div><div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:244px;max-width:244px"><div class="thumbimage" style="height:181px;overflow:hidden"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Animation_of_Galileo_trajectory_around_Jupiter.gif"><img alt="" data-file-height="420" data-file-width="560" decoding="async" height="182" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/Animation_of_Galileo_trajectory_around_Jupiter.gif/242px-Animation_of_Galileo_trajectory_around_Jupiter.gif" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/Animation_of_Galileo_trajectory_around_Jupiter.gif/363px-Animation_of_Galileo_trajectory_around_Jupiter.gif 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/Animation_of_Galileo_trajectory_around_Jupiter.gif/484px-Animation_of_Galileo_trajectory_around_Jupiter.gif 2x" width="242"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">Animation of <i>Galileo</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">\'s</span> trajectory around Jupiter from August 1, 1995, to September 30, 2003 <br/> <span style="margin:0; font-size:90%; white-space:nowrap;"><span class="legend-text" style="border:none; padding:1px .6em; background-color:magenta; color:black; font-size:95%; line-height:1.25; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;<i>Galileo</i></span>&#160;<b>&#183;</b> <span style="margin:0; font-size:90%; white-space:nowrap;"><span class="legend-text" style="border:none; padding:1px .6em; background-color:Lime; color:black; font-size:95%; line-height:1.25; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;<a href="/wiki/Jupiter" title="Jupiter">Jupiter</a></span>&#160;<b>&#183;</b> <span style="margin:0; font-size:90%; white-space:nowrap;"><span class="legend-text" style="border:none; padding:1px .6em; background-color:OrangeRed; color:black; font-size:95%; line-height:1.25; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;Io</span>&#160;<b>&#183;</b> <span style="margin:0; font-size:90%; white-space:nowrap;"><span class="legend-text" style="border:none; padding:1px .6em; background-color:RoyalBlue; color:black; font-size:95%; line-height:1.25; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;Europa</span>&#160;<b>&#183;</b> <span style="margin:0; font-size:90%; white-space:nowrap;"><span class="legend-text" style="border:none; padding:1px .6em; background-color:Gold; color:black; font-size:95%; line-height:1.25; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;<a href="/wiki/Ganymede_(moon)" title="Ganymede (moon)">Ganymede</a></span>&#160;<b>&#183;</b> <span style="margin:0; font-size:90%; white-space:nowrap;"><span class="legend-text" style="border:none; padding:1px .6em; background-color:Cyan; color:black; font-size:95%; line-height:1.25; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;<a href="/wiki/Callisto_(moon)" title="Callisto (moon)">Callisto</a></span></div></div><div style="clear:left"></div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Galileo_Europa_Mission_and_Prime_Mission_Tours.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="597" data-file-width="754" decoding="async" height="174" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/71/Galileo_Europa_Mission_and_Prime_Mission_Tours.png/220px-Galileo_Europa_Mission_and_Prime_Mission_Tours.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/71/Galileo_Europa_Mission_and_Prime_Mission_Tours.png/330px-Galileo_Europa_Mission_and_Prime_Mission_Tours.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/71/Galileo_Europa_Mission_and_Prime_Mission_Tours.png/440px-Galileo_Europa_Mission_and_Prime_Mission_Tours.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Galileo_Europa_Mission_and_Prime_Mission_Tours.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>Galileo</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">\'s</span> prime and extended mission orbits</div></div></div>\n<p><i>Galileo</i> flew by <a href="/wiki/Venus" title="Venus">Venus</a> at 05:58:48&#160;<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/UTC" title="UTC">UTC</a> on February 10, 1990, at a range of 16,106&#160;km (10,008&#160;mi). Having gained 8,030&#160;km/h (4,990&#160;mph) in speed, the spacecraft flew by <a href="/wiki/Earth" title="Earth">Earth</a> twice, the first time at a range of 960&#160;km (600&#160;mi) at 20:34:34&#160;UTC on December 8, 1990, before approaching the <a href="/wiki/S-type_asteroid" title="S-type asteroid">S-type asteroid</a> <a href="/wiki/951_Gaspra" title="951 Gaspra">951 Gaspra</a> to a distance of 1,604&#160;km (997&#160;mi) at 22:37&#160;UTC on October 29, 1991. <i>Galileo</i> then performed a second flyby of Earth at 303.1&#160;km (188.3&#160;mi) at 15:09:25&#160;UTC on December 8, 1992, adding 13,320&#160;km/h (8,280&#160;mph) to its cumulative speed. <i>Galileo</i> performed close observations of a second asteroid, <a href="/wiki/243_Ida" title="243 Ida">243 Ida</a>, at 16:51:59&#160;UTC on August 28, 1993, at a range of 2,410&#160;km (1,500&#160;mi). The spacecraft discovered Ida has a moon, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dactyl_(asteroid)" title="Dactyl (asteroid)">Dactyl</a>, the first discovery of a natural satellite orbiting an asteroid. In 1994, <i>Galileo</i> was perfectly positioned to watch the fragments of <a href="/wiki/Comet_Shoemaker%E2%80%93Levy_9" title="Comet Shoemaker&#8211;Levy 9">Comet Shoemaker&#8211;Levy 9</a> crash into Jupiter, whereas terrestrial telescopes had to wait to see the impact sites as they rotated into view. After releasing its <a href="/wiki/Galileo_Probe" title="Galileo Probe">atmospheric probe</a> on July 13, 1995, the <i>Galileo</i> orbiter became the first man-made satellite of Jupiter at 01:16&#160;UTC on December 8, 1995,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DAmario1992_3-3"><a href="#cite_note-DAmario1992-3">[3]</a></sup> after it fired its main engine to enter a 198-day parking orbit.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup>\n</p><p><i>Galileo</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">\'s</span> prime mission was a two-year study of the Jovian system. The spacecraft traveled around Jupiter in elongated <a href="/wiki/Ellipse" title="Ellipse">ellipses</a>, each orbit lasting about two months. The differing distances from Jupiter afforded by these orbits allowed <i>Galileo</i> to sample different parts of the planet\'s extensive <a href="/wiki/Magnetosphere" title="Magnetosphere">magnetosphere</a>. The orbits were designed for close-up flybys of Jupiter\'s largest moons. Once the prime mission concluded, an extended mission started on December 7, 1997; the spacecraft made several flybys of <a href="/wiki/Europa_(moon)" title="Europa (moon)">Europa</a> and <a href="/wiki/Io_(moon)" title="Io (moon)">Io</a>. The closest approach was 180&#160;km (110&#160;mi) on October 15, 2001. The <a href="/wiki/Radiation" title="Radiation">radiation</a> environment near Io was very unhealthy for <i>Galileo</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">\'s</span> systems, and so these flybys were saved for the extended mission when loss of the spacecraft would be more acceptable.\n</p><p><i>Galileo</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">\'s</span> cameras were deactivated on January 17, 2002, after they had sustained irreparable radiation damage. NASA engineers were able to recover the damaged tape recorder electronics, and <i>Galileo</i> continued to return scientific data until it was deorbited in 2003, performing one last scientific experiment: a measurement of the moon <a href="/wiki/Amalthea_(moon)" title="Amalthea (moon)">Amalthea</a>\'s mass as the spacecraft swung by it.\n</p><p>On December 11, 2013, NASA reported, based on results from the <i>Galileo</i> mission, the detection of "<a href="/wiki/Clay_minerals" title="Clay minerals">clay-like minerals</a>" (specifically, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Phyllosilicates" title="Phyllosilicates">phyllosilicates</a>), often associated with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Organic_materials" title="Organic materials">organic materials</a>, on the icy crust of <a href="/wiki/Europa_(moon)" title="Europa (moon)">Europa</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASA-20131211_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-NASA-20131211-10">[10]</a></sup> The presence of the minerals may have been the result of a collision with an <a href="/wiki/Asteroid" title="Asteroid">asteroid</a> or <a href="/wiki/Comet" title="Comet">comet</a>, according to the scientists.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASA-20131211_10-1"><a href="#cite_note-NASA-20131211-10">[10]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Spacecraft">Spacecraft</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Galileo_(spacecraft)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Spacecraft">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Galileo_Diagram.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="720" data-file-width="1041" decoding="async" height="152" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/45/Galileo_Diagram.jpg/220px-Galileo_Diagram.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/45/Galileo_Diagram.jpg/330px-Galileo_Diagram.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/45/Galileo_Diagram.jpg/440px-Galileo_Diagram.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Galileo_Diagram.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A diagram of <i>Galileo</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">\'s</span> main components</div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Galileo_in_1983.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1105" data-file-width="969" decoding="async" height="251" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/40/Galileo_in_1983.jpg/220px-Galileo_in_1983.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/40/Galileo_in_1983.jpg/330px-Galileo_in_1983.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/40/Galileo_in_1983.jpg/440px-Galileo_in_1983.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Galileo_in_1983.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>Galileo</i> with its main antenna open</div></div></div>\n<p>The <a href="/wiki/Jet_Propulsion_Laboratory" title="Jet Propulsion Laboratory">Jet Propulsion Laboratory</a> built the <i>Galileo</i> spacecraft and managed the <i>Galileo</i> mission for NASA. <a href="/wiki/West_Germany" title="West Germany">West Germany</a><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">\'s</span> <a href="/wiki/Messerschmitt-B%C3%B6lkow-Blohm" title="Messerschmitt-B&#246;lkow-Blohm">Messerschmitt-B&#246;lkow-Blohm</a> supplied the propulsion module. NASA\'s <a href="/wiki/Ames_Research_Center" title="Ames Research Center">Ames Research Center</a> managed the atmospheric probe, which was built by <a href="/wiki/Hughes_Aircraft_Company" title="Hughes Aircraft Company">Hughes Aircraft Company</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-galileo-arrival_2-13"><a href="#cite_note-galileo-arrival-2">[2]</a></sup>\n</p><p>At launch, the orbiter and probe together had a mass of 2,562&#160;kg (5,648&#160;lb) and stood 6.15&#160;m (20.2&#160;ft) tall.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-galileo-arrival_2-14"><a href="#cite_note-galileo-arrival-2">[2]</a></sup> One section of the spacecraft rotated at three&#160;<a href="/wiki/Revolutions_per_minute" title="Revolutions per minute">rpm</a>, keeping <i>Galileo</i> stable and holding six instruments that gathered data from many different directions, including the fields and particles instruments. The other section of the spacecraft was a 4.8-meter (16-foot) wide, umbrella-like high-gain antenna, and data were periodically transmitted to it. Back on the ground, the mission operations team used software containing 650,000 lines of programming code in the orbit sequence design process; 1,615,000 lines in the telemetry interpretation; and 550,000 lines of code in navigation.\n</p>\n<h3><span id="Command_and_Data_Handling_.28CDH.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Command_and_Data_Handling_(CDH)">Command and Data Handling (CDH)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Galileo_(spacecraft)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Command and Data Handling (CDH)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>The CDH subsystem was actively redundant, with two parallel <a href="/wiki/System_bus" title="System bus">data system buses</a> running at all times.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup> Each data system bus (a.k.a. string) was composed of the same functional elements, consisting of multiplexers (MUX), high-level modules (HLM), low-level modules (LLM), power converters (PC), bulk memory (BUM), data management subsystem bulk memory (DBUM), timing chains (TC), <a href="/wiki/Phase-locked_loop" title="Phase-locked loop">phase locked loops</a> (PLL), <a href="/wiki/Binary_Golay_code" title="Binary Golay code">Golay</a> coders (GC), hardware command decoders (HCD) and critical controllers (CRC).\n</p><p>The CDH subsystem was responsible for maintaining the following functions:\n</p>\n<ol><li>decoding of uplink commands</li>\n<li>execution of commands and sequences</li>\n<li>execution of system-level fault-protection responses</li>\n<li>collection, processing, and formatting of telemetry data for downlink transmission</li>\n<li>movement of data between subsystems via a data system bus</li></ol>\n<p>The spacecraft was controlled by six <a href="/wiki/RCA_1802" title="RCA 1802">RCA 1802</a> COSMAC <a href="/wiki/Microprocessor" title="Microprocessor">microprocessor</a> <a href="/wiki/Central_processing_unit" title="Central processing unit">CPUs</a>: four on the spun side and two on the despun side. Each CPU was clocked at about 1.6&#160;MHz, and fabricated on <a href="/wiki/Sapphire" title="Sapphire">sapphire</a> (<a href="/wiki/Silicon_on_sapphire" title="Silicon on sapphire">silicon on sapphire</a>), which is a <a href="/wiki/Radiation_hardening" title="Radiation hardening">radiation-and static-hardened</a> material ideal for spacecraft operation. This microprocessor was the first low-power <a href="/wiki/CMOS" title="CMOS">CMOS</a> processor chip, quite on a par with the 8-bit 6502 that was being built into the <a href="/wiki/Apple_II" title="Apple II">Apple II</a> <a href="/wiki/Desktop_computer" title="Desktop computer">desktop computer</a> at that time.\n</p><p>The Galileo Attitude and Articulation Control System (AACSE) was controlled by two <a href="/wiki/Itek" title="Itek">Itek</a> Advanced Technology Airborne Computers (ATAC), built using radiation-hardened <a href="/wiki/AMD_Am2900" title="AMD Am2900">2901s</a>. The AACSE could be reprogrammed in flight by sending the new program through the Command and Data Subsystem. <i>Galileo</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">\'s</span> attitude control system software was written in the <a href="/wiki/HAL/S" title="HAL/S">HAL/S</a> programming language,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-tom_7-2"><a href="#cite_note-tom-7">[7]</a></sup> also used in the <a href="/wiki/Space_Shuttle_program" title="Space Shuttle program">Space Shuttle program</a>.\n</p><p>Memory capacity provided by each BUM was 16K of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/RAM" title="RAM">RAM</a>, while the DBUMs each provided 8K of RAM. There were two BUMs and two DBUMs in the CDH subsystem and they all resided on the spun side of the spacecraft. The BUMs and DBUMs provided storage for sequences and contain various buffers for telemetry data and interbus communication.\n</p><p>Every HLM and LLM was built up around a single 1802 microprocessor and 32K of RAM (for HLMs) or 16K of RAM (for LLMs). Two HLMs and two LLMs resided on the spun side while two LLMs were on the despun side.\n</p><p>Thus, total memory capacity available to the CDH subsystem was 176K of RAM: 144K allocated to the spun side and 32K to the despun side.\n</p><p>Each HLM was responsible for the following functions:\n</p>\n<ol><li>uplink command processing</li>\n<li>maintenance of the spacecraft clock</li>\n<li>movement of data over the data system bus</li>\n<li>execution of stored sequences (time-event tables)</li>\n<li>telemetry control</li>\n<li>error recovery including system fault-protection monitoring and response</li></ol>\n<p>Each LLM was responsible for the following functions:\n</p>\n<ol><li>collect and format engineering data from the subsystems</li>\n<li>provide the capability to issue coded and discrete commands to spacecraft users</li>\n<li>recognize out-of-tolerance conditions on status inputs</li>\n<li>perform some system fault-protection functions</li></ol>\n<p>The HCD received command data from the modulation/demodulation subsystem, decoded these data and transferred them to the HLMs and CRCs.\n</p><p>The CRC controlled the configuration of CDH subsystem elements. It also controlled access to the two data system buses by other spacecraft subsystems. In addition, the CRC supplied signals to enable certain critical events (e.g. probe separation).\n</p><p>The GCs provided <a href="/wiki/Binary_Golay_code" title="Binary Golay code">Golay</a> encoding of data via hardware.\n</p><p>The TCs and PLLs established timing within the CDH subsystem.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Propulsion">Propulsion</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Galileo_(spacecraft)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Propulsion">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Galileo_propulsion_module.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="532" data-file-width="952" decoding="async" height="123" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c9/Galileo_propulsion_module.jpg/220px-Galileo_propulsion_module.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c9/Galileo_propulsion_module.jpg/330px-Galileo_propulsion_module.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c9/Galileo_propulsion_module.jpg/440px-Galileo_propulsion_module.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Galileo_propulsion_module.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>Galileo</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">\'s</span> propulsion module</div></div></div>\n<p>The propulsion subsystem consisted of a 400&#160;<a href="/wiki/Newton_(unit)" title="Newton (unit)">N</a> main engine and twelve 10&#160;N thrusters, together with propellant, storage and pressurizing tanks and associated plumbing. The 10&#160;N thrusters were mounted in groups of six on two 2-meter booms. The fuel for the system was 925&#160;kg (2,039&#160;lb) of <a href="/wiki/Monomethylhydrazine" title="Monomethylhydrazine">monomethylhydrazine</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nitrogen_tetroxide" title="Nitrogen tetroxide">nitrogen tetroxide</a>. Two separate tanks held another 7&#160;kg (15&#160;lb) of <a href="/wiki/Helium" title="Helium">helium</a> pressurant. The propulsion subsystem was developed and built by <a href="/wiki/Messerschmitt-B%C3%B6lkow-Blohm" title="Messerschmitt-B&#246;lkow-Blohm">Messerschmitt-B&#246;lkow-Blohm</a> and provided by West Germany, the major international partner in Project <i>Galileo</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Electrical_power">Electrical power</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Galileo_(spacecraft)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Electrical power">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>At the time, <a href="/wiki/Solar_panel" title="Solar panel">solar panels</a> were not practical at Jupiter\'s distance from the Sun; the spacecraft would have needed a minimum of 65 square meters (700&#160;sq&#160;ft) of panels. Chemical batteries would likewise be prohibitively large due to technological limitations. The solution was two <a href="/wiki/Radioisotope_thermoelectric_generator" title="Radioisotope thermoelectric generator">radioisotope thermoelectric generators</a> (RTGs) which powered the spacecraft through the radioactive decay of <a href="/wiki/Plutonium-238" title="Plutonium-238">plutonium-238</a>. The heat emitted by this decay was converted into electricity through the solid-state <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Seebeck_effect" title="Seebeck effect">Seebeck effect</a>. This provided a reliable and long-lasting source of electricity unaffected by the cold environment and high-radiation fields in the Jovian system.\n</p><p>Each <a href="/wiki/GPHS-RTG" title="GPHS-RTG">GPHS-RTG</a>, mounted on a 5-meter long (16&#160;ft) boom, carried 7.8 kilograms (17 pounds) of <span style="display:inline-block; margin-bottom:-0.3em; vertical-align:0.8em; line-height:1.2em; font-size:70%; text-align:left;">238<br/></span><a href="/wiki/Plutonium" title="Plutonium">Pu</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RTG_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-RTG-13">[13]</a></sup> Each RTG contained 18 separate heat source modules, and each module encased four pellets of <a href="/wiki/Plutonium(IV)_oxide" title="Plutonium(IV) oxide">plutonium(IV) oxide</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Ceramic" title="Ceramic">ceramic</a> material resistant to fracturing. The modules were designed to survive a range of potential accidents: launch vehicle explosion or fire, re-entry into the atmosphere followed by land or water impact, and post-impact situations. An outer covering of <a href="/wiki/Graphite" title="Graphite">graphite</a> provided protection against the structural, thermal, and eroding environments of a potential re-entry. Additional graphite components provided impact protection, while <a href="/wiki/Iridium" title="Iridium">iridium</a> cladding of the fuel cells provided post-impact containment. The RTGs produced about 570&#160;watts at launch. The power output initially decreased at the rate of 0.6&#160;watts per month and was 493&#160;watts when <i>Galileo</i> arrived at Jupiter.\n</p><p>As the launch of <i>Galileo</i> neared, anti-nuclear groups, concerned over what they perceived as an unacceptable risk to the public\'s safety from <i>Galileo</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">\'s</span> RTGs, sought a court injunction prohibiting <i>Galileo</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">\'s</span> launch. RTGs had been used for years in planetary exploration without mishap: the <a href="/wiki/Lincoln_Experimental_Satellite" title="Lincoln Experimental Satellite">Lincoln Experimental Satellites</a> 8/9, launched by the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Department_of_Defense" title="United States Department of Defense">U.S. Department of Defense</a>, had 7% more plutonium on board than <i>Galileo</i>, and the two <a href="/wiki/Voyager_program" title="Voyager program"><i>Voyager</i> spacecraft</a> each carried 80% as much plutonium as <i>Galileo</i>. Activists remembered the messy crash of the Soviet Union\'s nuclear-powered <a href="/wiki/Kosmos_954" title="Kosmos 954">Kosmos 954</a> satellite in Canada in 1978, and the 1986 <a href="/wiki/Space_Shuttle_Challenger_disaster" title="Space Shuttle Challenger disaster"><i>Challenger</i> accident</a>, which did not involve nuclear fuel but raised public awareness about spacecraft failures. In addition, no RTGs had ever done a non-orbital swing past the Earth at close range and high speed, as <i>Galileo</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">\'s</span> Venus-Earth-Earth gravity assist trajectory required it to do. This created a novel mission failure modality that might plausibly have entailed total dispersal of <i>Galileo</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">\'s</span> plutonium in the Earth\'s atmosphere. Scientist <a href="/wiki/Carl_Sagan" title="Carl Sagan">Carl Sagan</a>, for example, a strong supporter of the <i>Galileo</i> mission, said in 1989 that "there is nothing absurd about either side of this argument."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sagan_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sagan-14">[14]</a></sup>\n</p><p>After the <i>Challenger</i> accident, a study considered additional shielding but rejected it, in part because such a design significantly increased the overall risk of mission failure and only shifted the other risks around. For example, if a failure on orbit had occurred, additional shielding would have significantly increased the consequences of a ground impact.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RTG_13-1"><a href="#cite_note-RTG-13">[13]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Instrumentation_overview">Instrumentation overview</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Galileo_(spacecraft)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Instrumentation overview">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:PIA19048_realistic_color_Europa_mosaic_(alt).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1700" data-file-width="2300" decoding="async" height="163" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/93/PIA19048_realistic_color_Europa_mosaic_%28alt%29.jpg/220px-PIA19048_realistic_color_Europa_mosaic_%28alt%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/93/PIA19048_realistic_color_Europa_mosaic_%28alt%29.jpg/330px-PIA19048_realistic_color_Europa_mosaic_%28alt%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/93/PIA19048_realistic_color_Europa_mosaic_%28alt%29.jpg/440px-PIA19048_realistic_color_Europa_mosaic_%28alt%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:PIA19048_realistic_color_Europa_mosaic_(alt).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>This image of Europa was produced from the Galileo images of the moon</div></div></div>\n<p>Scientific instruments to measure fields and particles were mounted on the spinning section of the spacecraft, together with the main <a href="/wiki/Antenna_(radio)" title="Antenna (radio)">antenna</a>, power supply, the propulsion module and most of <i>Galileo</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">\'s</span> computers and control electronics. The sixteen instruments, weighing 118&#160;kg (260&#160;lb) altogether, included <a href="/wiki/Magnetometer" title="Magnetometer">magnetometer</a> sensors mounted on an 11&#160;m (36&#160;ft) boom to minimize interference from the spacecraft; a <a href="/wiki/Plasma_(physics)" title="Plasma (physics)">plasma</a> instrument for detecting low-energy charged particles and a plasma-wave detector to study waves generated by the particles; a high-energy particle detector; and a detector of cosmic and Jovian <a href="/wiki/Dust" title="Dust">dust</a>. It also carried the Heavy Ion Counter, an engineering experiment to assess the potentially hazardous charged particle environments the spacecraft flew through, and an <a href="/wiki/Extreme_ultraviolet" title="Extreme ultraviolet">extreme ultraviolet</a> detector associated with the UV spectrometer on the scan platform.\n</p><p>The despun section\'s instruments included the camera system; the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Near_infrared" title="Near infrared">near infrared</a> mapping spectrometer to make multi-spectral images for atmospheric and moon surface chemical analysis; the ultraviolet spectrometer to study gases; and the photopolarimeter-radiometer to measure radiant and reflected energy. The camera system was designed to obtain images of Jupiter\'s satellites at resolutions 20 to 1,000 times better than <a href="/wiki/Voyager_program" title="Voyager program"><i>Voyager</i></a><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">\'s</span> best, because <i>Galileo</i> flew closer to the planet and its inner moons, and because the more modern <a href="/wiki/Charge-coupled_device" title="Charge-coupled device">CCD</a> sensor in <i>Galileo</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">\'s</span> camera was more sensitive and had a broader color detection band than the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vidicon" title="Vidicon">vidicons</a> of <i>Voyager</i>.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Instrumentation_details">Instrumentation details</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Galileo_(spacecraft)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Instrumentation details">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>The following information was taken directly from NASA\'s <i>Galileo</i> legacy site.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Despun_section">Despun section</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Galileo_(spacecraft)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Despun section">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<h5><span id="Solid_State_Imager_.28SSI.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Solid_State_Imager_(SSI)">Solid State Imager (SSI)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Galileo_(spacecraft)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Solid State Imager (SSI)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h5>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Galileo_Solid-_State_Imaging.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="311" data-file-width="471" decoding="async" height="145" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a1/Galileo_Solid-_State_Imaging.jpg/220px-Galileo_Solid-_State_Imaging.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a1/Galileo_Solid-_State_Imaging.jpg/330px-Galileo_Solid-_State_Imaging.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a1/Galileo_Solid-_State_Imaging.jpg/440px-Galileo_Solid-_State_Imaging.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Galileo_Solid-_State_Imaging.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Solid-State Imager</div></div></div>\n<p>The SSI was an 800-by-800-pixel solid state camera consisting of an array of silicon sensors called a <a href="/wiki/Charge-coupled_device" title="Charge-coupled device">charge-coupled device</a> (CCD). <i>Galileo</i> was one of the first spacecraft to be equipped with a CCD camera.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2011)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>  The optical portion of the camera was built as a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cassegrain_telescope" title="Cassegrain telescope">Cassegrain telescope</a>. Light was collected by the primary mirror and directed to a smaller secondary mirror that channeled it through a hole in the center of the primary mirror and onto the CCD. The CCD sensor was shielded from <a href="/wiki/Radiation" title="Radiation">radiation</a>, a particular problem within the harsh Jovian magnetosphere. The shielding was accomplished by means of a 10&#160;mm (0.4&#160;in) thick layer of <a href="/wiki/Tantalum" title="Tantalum">tantalum</a> surrounding the CCD except where the light enters the system. An eight-position filter wheel was used to obtain images at specific wavelengths. The images were then combined electronically on Earth to produce color images. The spectral response of the SSI ranged from about 0.4 to 1.1&#160;micrometers. The SSI weighed 29.7&#160;kg (65&#160;lb) and consumed, on average, 15 watts of power.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h5><span id="Near-Infrared_Mapping_Spectrometer_.28NIMS.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Near-Infrared_Mapping_Spectrometer_(NIMS)">Near-Infrared Mapping Spectrometer (NIMS)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Galileo_(spacecraft)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Near-Infrared Mapping Spectrometer (NIMS)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h5>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Galileo_-_NIMS_photo_-_nims1.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="420" data-file-width="576" decoding="async" height="160" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c8/Galileo_-_NIMS_photo_-_nims1.jpg/220px-Galileo_-_NIMS_photo_-_nims1.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c8/Galileo_-_NIMS_photo_-_nims1.jpg/330px-Galileo_-_NIMS_photo_-_nims1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c8/Galileo_-_NIMS_photo_-_nims1.jpg/440px-Galileo_-_NIMS_photo_-_nims1.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Galileo_-_NIMS_photo_-_nims1.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Near-Infrared Mapping Spectrometer</div></div></div>\n<p>The NIMS instrument was sensitive to 0.7-to-5.2-<a href="/wiki/Micrometer" title="Micrometer">micrometer</a> wavelength <a href="/wiki/Infrared" title="Infrared">infrared</a> light, overlapping the wavelength range of the SSI. The telescope associated with NIMS was all reflective (using only mirrors and no lenses) with an aperture of 229&#160;mm (9&#160;in). The <a href="/wiki/Spectrometer" title="Spectrometer">spectrometer</a> of NIMS used a grating to disperse the light collected by the telescope. The dispersed spectrum of light was focused on detectors of <a href="/wiki/Indium" title="Indium">indium</a>, <a href="/wiki/Antimonide" title="Antimonide">antimonide</a> and <a href="/wiki/Silicon" title="Silicon">silicon</a>. The NIMS weighed 18&#160;kg (40&#160;lb) and used 12 watts of power on average.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h5><span id="Ultraviolet_Spectrometer_.2F_Extreme_Ultraviolet_Spectrometer_.28UVS.2FEUV.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Ultraviolet_Spectrometer_/_Extreme_Ultraviolet_Spectrometer_(UVS/EUV)">Ultraviolet Spectrometer / Extreme Ultraviolet Spectrometer (UVS/EUV)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Galileo_(spacecraft)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Ultraviolet Spectrometer / Extreme Ultraviolet Spectrometer (UVS/EUV)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h5>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Galileo_-_UVS_photo_-_uvs1.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="568" data-file-width="697" decoding="async" height="179" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/55/Galileo_-_UVS_photo_-_uvs1.jpg/220px-Galileo_-_UVS_photo_-_uvs1.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/55/Galileo_-_UVS_photo_-_uvs1.jpg/330px-Galileo_-_UVS_photo_-_uvs1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/55/Galileo_-_UVS_photo_-_uvs1.jpg/440px-Galileo_-_UVS_photo_-_uvs1.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Galileo_-_UVS_photo_-_uvs1.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Ultraviolet Spectrometer</div></div></div>\n<p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cassegrain_telescope" title="Cassegrain telescope">Cassegrain telescope</a> of the UVS had a 250&#160;mm (9.8&#160;in) aperture and collected light from the observation target. Both the UVS and EUV instruments used a ruled <a href="/wiki/Grating" title="Grating">grating</a> to disperse this light for spectral analysis. This light then passed through an exit slit into <a href="/wiki/Photomultiplier" title="Photomultiplier">photomultiplier</a> tubes that produced pulses or "sprays" of electrons. These electron pulses were counted, and these count numbers constituted the data that were sent to Earth. The UVS was mounted on <i>Galileo</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">\'s</span> scan platform and could be pointed to an object in inertial space. The EUV was mounted on the spun section. As <i>Galileo</i> rotated, EUV observed a narrow ribbon of space perpendicular to the spin axis. The two instruments combined weighed about 9.7&#160;kg (21&#160;lb) and used 5.9 watts of power.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h5><span id="Photopolarimeter-Radiometer_.28PPR.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Photopolarimeter-Radiometer_(PPR)">Photopolarimeter-Radiometer (PPR)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Galileo_(spacecraft)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Photopolarimeter-Radiometer (PPR)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h5>\n<p>The PPR had seven radiometry bands. One of these used no filters and observed all incoming radiation, both solar and thermal. Another band allowed only solar radiation through. The difference between the solar-plus-thermal and the solar-only channels gave the total thermal radiation emitted. The PPR also measured in five broadband channels that spanned the spectral range from 17 to 110 micrometers. The radiometer provided data on the temperatures of Jupiter\'s atmosphere and satellites. The design of the instrument was based on that of an instrument flown on the <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pioneer_Venus" title="Pioneer Venus">Pioneer Venus</a></i> spacecraft. A 100&#160;mm (4&#160;in) aperture reflecting telescope collected light and directed it to a series of filters, and, from there, measurements were performed by the detectors of the PPR. The PPR weighed 5.0&#160;kg (11.0&#160;lb) and consumed about 5 watts of power.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Spun_section">Spun section</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Galileo_(spacecraft)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Spun section">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<h5><span id="Dust_Detector_Subsystem_.28DDS.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Dust_Detector_Subsystem_(DDS)">Dust Detector Subsystem (DDS)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Galileo_(spacecraft)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Dust Detector Subsystem (DDS)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h5>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Galileo_-_DDS_photo_-_dds1.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="378" data-file-width="576" decoding="async" height="144" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/Galileo_-_DDS_photo_-_dds1.jpg/220px-Galileo_-_DDS_photo_-_dds1.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/Galileo_-_DDS_photo_-_dds1.jpg/330px-Galileo_-_DDS_photo_-_dds1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/Galileo_-_DDS_photo_-_dds1.jpg/440px-Galileo_-_DDS_photo_-_dds1.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Galileo_-_DDS_photo_-_dds1.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Dust Detector Subsystem</div></div></div>\n<p>The Dust Detector Subsystem (DDS) was used to measure the mass, electric charge, and velocity of incoming particles. The masses of dust particles that the DDS could detect go from 10<sup>&#8722;<span style="white-space:nowrap">16</span></sup> to 10<sup>&#8722;<span style="white-space:nowrap">7</span></sup> grams. The speed of these small particles could be measured over the range of 1 to 70 kilometers per second (0.6 to 43.5&#160;mi/s). The instrument could measure impact rates from 1 particle per 115 days (10 megaseconds) to 100 particles per second. Such data was used to help determine dust origin and dynamics within the <a href="/wiki/Magnetosphere" title="Magnetosphere">magnetosphere</a>. The DDS weighed 4.2&#160;kg (9.3&#160;lb) and used an average of 5.4 watts of power.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h5><span id="Energetic_Particles_Detector_.28EPD.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Energetic_Particles_Detector_(EPD)">Energetic Particles Detector (EPD)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Galileo_(spacecraft)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Energetic Particles Detector (EPD)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h5>\n<p>The Energetic Particles Detector (EPD) was designed to measure the numbers and energies of ions and electrons whose energies exceeded about 20&#160;<a href="/wiki/Electronvolt" title="Electronvolt">keV</a> (3.2&#160;<a href="/wiki/Joule" title="Joule">fJ</a>). The EPD could also measure the direction of travel of such particles and, in the case of ions, could determine their composition (whether the ion is <a href="/wiki/Oxygen" title="Oxygen">oxygen</a> or <a href="/wiki/Sulfur" title="Sulfur">sulfur</a>, for example). The EPD used silicon solid-state detectors and a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Time-of-flight" title="Time-of-flight">time-of-flight</a> detector system to measure changes in the energetic particle population at Jupiter as a function of position and time. These measurements helped determine how the particles got their energy and how they were transported through Jupiter\'s magnetosphere. The EPD weighed 10.5&#160;kg (23&#160;lb) and used 10.1 watts of power on average.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h5><span id="Heavy_Ion_Counter_.28HIC.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Heavy_Ion_Counter_(HIC)">Heavy Ion Counter (HIC)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Galileo_(spacecraft)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Heavy Ion Counter (HIC)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h5>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Galileo_Heavy_Ion_Counter.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="550" data-file-width="905" decoding="async" height="134" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/25/Galileo_Heavy_Ion_Counter.jpg/220px-Galileo_Heavy_Ion_Counter.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/25/Galileo_Heavy_Ion_Counter.jpg/330px-Galileo_Heavy_Ion_Counter.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/25/Galileo_Heavy_Ion_Counter.jpg/440px-Galileo_Heavy_Ion_Counter.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Galileo_Heavy_Ion_Counter.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Heavy Ion Counter</div></div></div>\n<p>The HIC was, in effect, a repackaged and updated version of some parts of the flight spare of the <i><a href="/wiki/Voyager_program" title="Voyager program">Voyager</a></i> Cosmic Ray System. The HIC detected heavy <a href="/wiki/Ion" title="Ion">ions</a> using stacks of single crystal silicon wafers. The HIC could measure heavy ions with energies as low as 6&#160;MeV (1&#160;pJ) and as high as 200&#160;MeV (32&#160;pJ) per nucleon. This range included all atomic substances between <a href="/wiki/Carbon" title="Carbon">carbon</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nickel" title="Nickel">nickel</a>. The HIC and the EUV shared a communications link and, therefore, had to share observing time. The HIC weighed 8.0&#160;kg (17.6&#160;lb) and used an average of 2.8 watts of power.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h5><span id="Magnetometer_.28MAG.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Magnetometer_(MAG)">Magnetometer (MAG)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Galileo_(spacecraft)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Magnetometer (MAG)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h5>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Galileo_-_MAD_photo_-_mag1.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="381" data-file-width="576" decoding="async" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0d/Galileo_-_MAD_photo_-_mag1.jpg/220px-Galileo_-_MAD_photo_-_mag1.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0d/Galileo_-_MAD_photo_-_mag1.jpg/330px-Galileo_-_MAD_photo_-_mag1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0d/Galileo_-_MAD_photo_-_mag1.jpg/440px-Galileo_-_MAD_photo_-_mag1.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Galileo_-_MAD_photo_-_mag1.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Magnetometer</div></div></div>\n<p>The <a href="/wiki/Spacecraft_magnetometer" title="Spacecraft magnetometer">magnetometer</a> (MAG) used two sets of three sensors. The three sensors allowed the three orthogonal components of the <a href="/wiki/Magnetic_field" title="Magnetic field">magnetic field</a> section to be measured. One set was located at the end of the magnetometer boom and, in that position, was about 11&#160;m (36&#160;ft) from the spin axis of the spacecraft. The second set, designed to detect stronger fields, was 6.7&#160;m (22&#160;ft) from the spin axis. The boom was used to remove the MAG from the immediate vicinity of <i>Galileo</i> to minimize magnetic effects from the spacecraft. However, not all these effects could be eliminated by distancing the instrument. The rotation of the spacecraft was used to separate natural magnetic fields from engineering-induced fields. Another source of potential error in measurement came from the bending and twisting of the long magnetometer boom. To account for these motions, a calibration coil was mounted rigidly on the spacecraft to generate a reference magnetic field during calibrations. The magnetic field at the surface of the Earth has a strength of about 50,000&#160;<a href="/wiki/Tesla_(unit)" title="Tesla (unit)">nT</a>. At Jupiter, the outboard (11 m) set of sensors could measure magnetic field strengths in the range from &#177;32 to &#177;512&#160;nT, while the inboard (6.7 m) set was active in the range from &#177;512 to &#177;16,384&#160;nT. The MAG experiment weighed 7.0&#160;kg (15.4&#160;lb) and used 3.9 watts of power.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Galileo_-_PWS_photo_-_pws1.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="356" data-file-width="540" decoding="async" height="145" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6f/Galileo_-_PWS_photo_-_pws1.jpg/220px-Galileo_-_PWS_photo_-_pws1.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6f/Galileo_-_PWS_photo_-_pws1.jpg/330px-Galileo_-_PWS_photo_-_pws1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6f/Galileo_-_PWS_photo_-_pws1.jpg/440px-Galileo_-_PWS_photo_-_pws1.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Galileo_-_PWS_photo_-_pws1.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Plasma Wave Subsystem</div></div></div>\n<h5><span id="Plasma_Subsystem_.28PLS.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Plasma_Subsystem_(PLS)">Plasma Subsystem (PLS)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Galileo_(spacecraft)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Plasma Subsystem (PLS)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h5>\n<p>The PLS used seven fields of view to collect <a href="/wiki/Particle_radiation" title="Particle radiation">charged particles</a> for energy and mass analysis. These fields of view covered most angles from 0 to 180&#160;degrees, fanning out from the spin axis. The rotation of the spacecraft carried each field of view through a full circle. The PLS measured particles in the energy range from 0.9 to 52,000&#160;<a href="/wiki/Electronvolt" title="Electronvolt">eV</a> (0.14 to 8,300&#160;<a href="/wiki/Joule" title="Joule">aJ</a>). The PLS weighed 13.2&#160;kg (29&#160;lb) and used an average of 10.7 watts of power.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h5><span id="Plasma_Wave_Subsystem_.28PWS.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Plasma_Wave_Subsystem_(PWS)">Plasma Wave Subsystem (PWS)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Galileo_(spacecraft)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Plasma Wave Subsystem (PWS)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h5>\n<p>An electric <a href="/wiki/Dipole_antenna" title="Dipole antenna">dipole antenna</a> was used to study the electric fields of <a href="/wiki/Plasma_(physics)" title="Plasma (physics)">plasmas</a>, while two search coil magnetic antennas studied the magnetic fields. The electric dipole antenna was mounted at the tip of the magnetometer boom. The search coil magnetic antennas were mounted on the high-gain antenna feed. Nearly simultaneous measurements of the electric and magnetic field spectrum allowed <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electrostatic_wave" title="Electrostatic wave">electrostatic waves</a> to be distinguished from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electromagnetic_wave" title="Electromagnetic wave">electromagnetic waves</a>. The PWS weighed 7.1&#160;kg (16&#160;lb) and used an average of 9.8 watts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div style="clear:both;"></div>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Galileo_Probe"><i>Galileo</i> Probe</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Galileo_(spacecraft)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Galileo Probe">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r856303569" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="thumb tmulti tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:286px;max-width:286px"><div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:125px;max-width:125px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Galileo_probe_-_artistic_impression_-_AC89-0146-3.png"><img alt="" data-file-height="2236" data-file-width="1796" decoding="async" height="153" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/12/Galileo_probe_-_artistic_impression_-_AC89-0146-3.png/123px-Galileo_probe_-_artistic_impression_-_AC89-0146-3.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/12/Galileo_probe_-_artistic_impression_-_AC89-0146-3.png/185px-Galileo_probe_-_artistic_impression_-_AC89-0146-3.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/12/Galileo_probe_-_artistic_impression_-_AC89-0146-3.png/246px-Galileo_probe_-_artistic_impression_-_AC89-0146-3.png 2x" width="123"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">Illustration of the <i>Galileo</i> Probe</div></div><div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:157px;max-width:157px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Galileo_Probe_diagram.jpeg"><img alt="" data-file-height="1513" data-file-width="1534" decoding="async" height="153" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a3/Galileo_Probe_diagram.jpeg/155px-Galileo_Probe_diagram.jpeg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a3/Galileo_Probe_diagram.jpeg/233px-Galileo_Probe_diagram.jpeg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a3/Galileo_Probe_diagram.jpeg/310px-Galileo_Probe_diagram.jpeg 2x" width="155"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">Diagram of the <i>Galileo</i> Probe sans parachute</div></div><div style="clear:left"></div></div></div>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Galileo_Probe" title="Galileo Probe">Galileo Probe</a></div>\n<p>The <b><i>Galileo</i> Probe</b> was an atmospheric-entry probe carried by the main <i>Galileo</i> spacecraft on its way to <a href="/wiki/Jupiter" title="Jupiter">Jupiter</a>. It separated from the main spacecraft on July 10, 1995, five months before its rendezvous with the planet on December 7.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DAmario1992_3-4"><a href="#cite_note-DAmario1992-3">[3]</a></sup> After a rough deceleration, the Descent Module started to return data to the main spacecraft orbiting high above Jupiter.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gpr_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-gpr-36">[36]</a></sup> The 339-kilogram (747&#160;lb) probe was built by <a href="/wiki/Hughes_Aircraft_Company" title="Hughes Aircraft Company">Hughes Aircraft Company</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup> at its <a href="/wiki/El_Segundo,_California" title="El Segundo, California">El Segundo, California</a> plant and measured about 1.3 meters (4.3&#160;ft) across. Inside the probe\'s <a href="/wiki/Heat_shield" title="Heat shield">heat shield</a>, the Descent Module with its scientific instruments was protected from extreme heat and pressure during its high-speed journey into the Jovian atmosphere, entering at 47.8 kilometers per second (29.7&#160;mi/s)\n</p><p>During the 57 minutes of data collecting, the <i>Galileo</i> Probe returned some surprising data on Jupiter\'s atmospheric conditions and composition and achieved some new discoveries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gpr_36-1"><a href="#cite_note-gpr-36">[36]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Jupiter_science">Jupiter science</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Galileo_(spacecraft)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Jupiter science">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Timeline_of_Galileo_(spacecraft)" title="Timeline of Galileo (spacecraft)">Timeline of Galileo (spacecraft)</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Io_-_Tvashtar_Catena.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="416" data-file-width="800" decoding="async" height="114" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/ff/Io_-_Tvashtar_Catena.jpg/220px-Io_-_Tvashtar_Catena.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/ff/Io_-_Tvashtar_Catena.jpg/330px-Io_-_Tvashtar_Catena.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/ff/Io_-_Tvashtar_Catena.jpg/440px-Io_-_Tvashtar_Catena.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Io_-_Tvashtar_Catena.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Tvashtar Catena on Io, showing changes in hot spots between 1999 and 2000</div></div></div>\n<p>After arriving on December 8, 1995 (UTC), and completing 35 orbits around Jupiter throughout a nearly eight-year mission, the <i>Galileo</i> orbiter was destroyed during a controlled impact with Jupiter on September 21, 2003. During that intervening time, <i>Galileo</i> changed the way scientists saw Jupiter and provided a wealth of information on the moons orbiting the planet which will be studied for years to come. Culled from NASA\'s press kit, the top orbiter scientific results were:\n</p>\n<ul><li><i>Galileo</i> made the first observation of <a href="/wiki/Ammonia" title="Ammonia">ammonia</a> clouds in another planet\'s atmosphere. The atmosphere creates ammonia ice particles from material coming up from lower depths.</li>\n<li>The moon <a href="/wiki/Io_(moon)" title="Io (moon)">Io</a> was confirmed to have extensive volcanic activity that is 100 times greater than that found on Earth. The heat and frequency of eruptions are reminiscent of early Earth.</li>\n<li>Complex plasma interactions in Io\'s atmosphere create immense electrical currents which couple to Jupiter\'s atmosphere.</li>\n<li>Several lines of evidence from <i>Galileo</i> support the theory that liquid oceans exist under <a href="/wiki/Europa_(moon)" title="Europa (moon)">Europa</a>\'s icy surface.</li>\n<li>Ganymede possesses its own, substantial magnetic field &#8211; the first satellite known to have one.</li>\n<li><i>Galileo</i> magnetic data provided evidence that Europa, <a href="/wiki/Ganymede_(moon)" title="Ganymede (moon)">Ganymede</a> and <a href="/wiki/Callisto_(moon)" title="Callisto (moon)">Callisto</a> have a liquid salt water layer under the visible surface.</li>\n<li>Evidence exists that Europa, Ganymede, and Callisto all have a thin atmospheric layer known as a \'surface-bound <a href="/wiki/Exosphere" title="Exosphere">exosphere</a>\'.</li>\n<li>Jupiter\'s <a href="/wiki/Ring_system" title="Ring system">ring system</a> is formed by dust kicked up as interplanetary <a href="/wiki/Meteoroid" title="Meteoroid">meteoroids</a> smash into the planet\'s <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Inner_satellites_of_Jupiter" title="Inner satellites of Jupiter">four small inner moons</a>. The outermost ring is actually two rings, one embedded with the other. There is probably a separate ring along <a href="/wiki/Amalthea_(moon)" title="Amalthea (moon)">Amalthea</a>\'s orbit, as well.</li>\n<li>The <i>Galileo</i> spacecraft identified the global structure and dynamics of a giant planet\'s <a href="/wiki/Magnetosphere" title="Magnetosphere">magnetosphere</a>.</li></ul>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_science_conducted_by_Galileo">Other science conducted by <i>Galileo</i></span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Galileo_(spacecraft)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Other science conducted by Galileo">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Remote_detection_of_life_on_Earth">Remote detection of life on Earth</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Galileo_(spacecraft)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Remote detection of life on Earth">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Galileo_Earth_-_PIA00114.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="800" data-file-width="800" decoding="async" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0b/Galileo_Earth_-_PIA00114.jpg/220px-Galileo_Earth_-_PIA00114.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0b/Galileo_Earth_-_PIA00114.jpg/330px-Galileo_Earth_-_PIA00114.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0b/Galileo_Earth_-_PIA00114.jpg/440px-Galileo_Earth_-_PIA00114.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Galileo_Earth_-_PIA00114.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>Galileo</i> image of Earth, taken in December 1990</div></div></div>\n<p>The astronomer <a href="/wiki/Carl_Sagan" title="Carl Sagan">Carl Sagan</a>, pondering the question of whether life on Earth could be easily detected from space, devised a set of experiments in the late 1980s using <i>Galileo</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">\'s</span> remote sensing instruments during the mission\'s first Earth flyby in December 1990. After data acquisition and processing, Sagan <i>et al.</i> published a paper in <i><a href="/wiki/Nature_(journal)" title="Nature (journal)">Nature</a></i> in 1993 detailing the results of the experiment. <i>Galileo</i> had indeed found what are now referred to as the "Sagan criteria for life". These included strong absorption of light at the red end of the visible spectrum (especially over <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Continents" title="Continents">continents</a>) which was caused by absorption by chlorophyll in photosynthesizing plants, absorption bands of molecular oxygen which is also a result of plant activity, infrared absorption bands caused by the ~1 micromole per <a href="/wiki/Mole_(unit)" title="Mole (unit)">mole</a> (&#181;mol/mol) of methane in Earth\'s atmosphere (a gas which must be replenished by either volcanic or biological activity), and modulated narrowband radio wave transmissions uncharacteristic of any known natural source. <i>Galileo</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">\'s</span> experiments were thus the first ever controls in the newborn science of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Astrobiological" title="Astrobiological">astrobiological</a> remote sensing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Galileo_Optical_Experiment"><i>Galileo</i> Optical Experiment</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Galileo_(spacecraft)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Galileo Optical Experiment">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:GOPEX(galileo_optical_experiment).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="613" data-file-width="650" decoding="async" height="207" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/65/GOPEX%28galileo_optical_experiment%29.jpg/220px-GOPEX%28galileo_optical_experiment%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/65/GOPEX%28galileo_optical_experiment%29.jpg/330px-GOPEX%28galileo_optical_experiment%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/65/GOPEX%28galileo_optical_experiment%29.jpg/440px-GOPEX%28galileo_optical_experiment%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:GOPEX(galileo_optical_experiment).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>An image of Earth taken by <i>Galileo</i> during the GOPEX test, showing laser
