pulses coming from a transmitting telescope on the night side. <i>Galileo</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">\'s</span> imager was panned downward during the exposure to separate the pulses, thus blurring Earth\'s image on the right.</div></div></div>\n<p>In December 1992, during <i>Galileo</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">\'s</span> second gravity-assist <a href="/wiki/Planetary_flyby" title="Planetary flyby">planetary flyby</a> of Earth, another groundbreaking experiment was performed. Optical communications in space was assessed by detecting light pulses from powerful lasers with <i>Galileo</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">\'s</span> <a href="/wiki/Charge-coupled_device" title="Charge-coupled device">CCD</a>. The experiment, dubbed <i>Galileo</i> Optical Experiment or GOPEX,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup> used two separate sites to beam laser pulses to the spacecraft, one at <a href="/wiki/Table_Mountain_Observatory" title="Table Mountain Observatory">Table Mountain Observatory</a> in California and the other at the <a href="/wiki/Starfire_Optical_Range" title="Starfire Optical Range">Starfire Optical Range</a> in New Mexico. The Table Mountain site used a <a href="/wiki/Nonlinear_optics" title="Nonlinear optics">frequency doubled</a> <a href="/wiki/Neodymium" title="Neodymium">neodymium</a>-<a href="/wiki/Yttrium" title="Yttrium">yttrium</a>-<a href="/wiki/Aluminium" title="Aluminium">aluminium</a> <a href="/wiki/Garnet" title="Garnet">garnet</a> (<a href="/wiki/Nd:YAG_laser" title="Nd:YAG laser">Nd:YAG</a>) laser operating at 532&#160;nm with a repetition rate of ~15 to 30&#160;Hz and a pulse power (<a href="/wiki/Full_width_at_half_maximum" title="Full width at half maximum">FWHM</a>) in the tens of megawatts range, which was coupled to a 0.6&#160;m (2.0&#160;ft) Cassegrain telescope for transmission to <i>Galileo</i>. The Starfire range site used a similar setup with a larger, 1.5&#160;m (4.9&#160;ft), transmitting telescope. Long exposure (~0.1 to 0.8&#160;s) images using <i>Galileo</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">\'s</span> 560&#160;nm centered green filter produced images of Earth clearly showing the laser pulses even at distances of up to 6&#160;million&#160;km (3.7&#160;million&#160;mi). Adverse weather conditions, restrictions placed on laser transmissions by the U.S. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Space_Defense_Operations_Center" title="Space Defense Operations Center">Space Defense Operations Center</a> (<a href="/wiki/Cheyenne_Mountain_Complex" title="Cheyenne Mountain Complex">SPADOC</a>) and a pointing error caused by the scan platform acceleration on the spacecraft being slower than expected (which prevented laser detection on all frames with less than 400&#160;ms exposure times) all contributed to the reduction of the number of successful detections of the laser transmission to 48 of the total 159 frames taken. Nonetheless, the experiment was considered a resounding success and the data acquired will likely be used in the future to design laser "downlinks" which will send large volumes of data very quickly from spacecraft to Earth. The scheme was already being studied (as of 2004) for a data link to a future Mars orbiting spacecraft.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Lunar_observations">Lunar observations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Galileo_(spacecraft)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Lunar observations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<ul class="gallery mw-gallery-traditional center">\n<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 235px"><div style="width: 235px">\n<div class="thumb" style="width: 230px;"><div style="margin:15px auto;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:The_Moon_from_Galileo_-_GPN-2000-000473.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="2871" data-file-width="2543" decoding="async" height="200" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/af/The_Moon_from_Galileo_-_GPN-2000-000473.jpg/177px-The_Moon_from_Galileo_-_GPN-2000-000473.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/af/The_Moon_from_Galileo_-_GPN-2000-000473.jpg/266px-The_Moon_from_Galileo_-_GPN-2000-000473.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/af/The_Moon_from_Galileo_-_GPN-2000-000473.jpg/354px-The_Moon_from_Galileo_-_GPN-2000-000473.jpg 2x" width="177"/></a></div></div>\n<div class="gallerytext">\n<p><i>Galileo</i> shot of Earth\'s Moon\n</p>\n</div>\n</div></li>\n<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 235px"><div style="width: 235px">\n<div class="thumb" style="width: 230px;"><div style="margin:15px auto;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Moon_Crescent_-_False_Color_Mosaic.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="2910" data-file-width="2600" decoding="async" height="200" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/Moon_Crescent_-_False_Color_Mosaic.jpg/179px-Moon_Crescent_-_False_Color_Mosaic.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/Moon_Crescent_-_False_Color_Mosaic.jpg/268px-Moon_Crescent_-_False_Color_Mosaic.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/Moon_Crescent_-_False_Color_Mosaic.jpg/357px-Moon_Crescent_-_False_Color_Mosaic.jpg 2x" width="179"/></a></div></div>\n<div class="gallerytext">\n<p><a href="/wiki/False_color" title="False color">False color</a> mosaic by <i>Galileo</i> showing <a href="/wiki/Geology_of_the_Moon" title="Geology of the Moon">compositional variations</a> of the Moon\'s surface\n</p>\n</div>\n</div></li>\n</ul>\n<div style="clear:both;"></div>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Star_scanner">Star scanner</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Galileo_(spacecraft)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Star scanner">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p><i>Galileo</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">\'s</span> star scanner was a small optical telescope that provided an absolute attitude reference. It also made several scientific discoveries serendipitously.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup> In the prime mission, it was found that the star scanner was able to detect high-energy particles as a noise signal. This data was eventually calibrated to show the particles were predominantly &gt;2&#160;MeV (0.32&#160;pJ) electrons that were trapped in the Jovian magnetic belts, and released to the Planetary Data System.\n</p><p>A second discovery occurred in 2000. The star scanner was observing a set of stars which included the second magnitude star <a href="/wiki/Delta_Velorum" title="Delta Velorum">Delta Velorum</a>. At one point, this star dimmed for 8 hours below the star scanner\'s detection threshold. Subsequent analysis of <i>Galileo</i> data and work by amateur and professional astronomers showed that Delta Velorum is the brightest known <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eclipsing_binary" title="Eclipsing binary">eclipsing binary</a>, brighter at maximum than even <a href="/wiki/Algol" title="Algol">Algol</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup> It has a primary period of 45 days and the dimming is just visible with the naked eye.\n</p><p>A final discovery occurred during the last two orbits of the mission. When the spacecraft passed the orbit of Jupiter\'s moon <a href="/wiki/Amalthea_(moon)" title="Amalthea (moon)">Amalthea</a>, the star scanner detected unexpected flashes of light that were reflections from moonlets. None of the individual moonlets were reliably sighted twice, hence no orbits were determined and the moonlets did not meet the International Astronomical Union requirements to receive designations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IAU_2003_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-IAU_2003-43">[43]</a></sup> It is believed that these moonlets most likely are debris ejected from Amalthea and form a tenuous, and perhaps temporary, ring.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Asteroid_encounters">Asteroid encounters</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Galileo_(spacecraft)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Asteroid encounters">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r856303569" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="thumb tmulti tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:331px;max-width:331px"><div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:152px;max-width:152px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:951_Gaspra.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="785" data-file-width="945" decoding="async" height="125" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/81/951_Gaspra.jpg/150px-951_Gaspra.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/81/951_Gaspra.jpg/225px-951_Gaspra.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/81/951_Gaspra.jpg/300px-951_Gaspra.jpg 2x" width="150"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left"><a href="/wiki/951_Gaspra" title="951 Gaspra">951 Gaspra</a> (enhanced colorization)</div></div><div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:175px;max-width:175px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:243_ida.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="520" data-file-width="718" decoding="async" height="125" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7e/243_ida.jpg/173px-243_ida.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7e/243_ida.jpg/260px-243_ida.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7e/243_ida.jpg/346px-243_ida.jpg 2x" width="173"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left"><a href="/wiki/243_Ida" title="243 Ida">243 Ida</a>, with its moon Dactyl to the right</div></div><div style="clear:left"></div></div></div>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="First_asteroid_encounter:_951_Gaspra">First asteroid encounter: 951 Gaspra</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Galileo_(spacecraft)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: First asteroid encounter: 951 Gaspra">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>On October 29, 1991, two months after entering the asteroid belt, <i>Galileo</i> performed the first asteroid encounter by a spacecraft, passing approximately 1,600 kilometers (990&#160;mi) from <a href="/wiki/951_Gaspra" title="951 Gaspra">951 Gaspra</a> at a relative speed of about 8 kilometers per second (5.0&#160;mi/s). Several pictures of Gaspra were taken, along with measurements using the NIMS instrument to indicate composition and physical properties. The last two images were relayed back to Earth in November 1991 and June 1992. The imagery revealed a cratered and very irregular body, measuring about 19 by 12 by 11 kilometers (11.8 by 7.5 by 6.8 miles). The remainder of data taken, including low-resolution images of more of the surface, were transmitted in late November 1992.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Second_asteroid_encounter:_243_Ida_and_Dactyl">Second asteroid encounter: 243 Ida and Dactyl</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Galileo_(spacecraft)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Second asteroid encounter: 243 Ida and Dactyl">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/243_Ida#Galileo_flyby" title="243 Ida">243 Ida</a></div>\n<p>On August 28, 1993, <i>Galileo</i> flew within 2,400 kilometers (1,500&#160;mi) of the asteroid <a href="/wiki/243_Ida" title="243 Ida">243 Ida</a>. The probe discovered that Ida had a small moon, dubbed Dactyl, measuring around 1.4 kilometers (0.87&#160;mi) in diameter; this was the first <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Asteroid_moon" title="Asteroid moon">asteroid moon</a> discovered. Measurements using <i>Galileo</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">\'s</span> Solid State Imager, Magnetometer and NIMS instrument were taken. From subsequent analysis of this data, Dactyl appears to be an SII-subtype S-type asteroid, and is spectrally different from 243&#160;Ida. It is hypothesized that Dactyl may have been produced by partial melting within a <a href="/wiki/Koronis_family" title="Koronis family">Koronis</a> parent body, while the 243&#160;Ida region escaped such igneous processing.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Mission_challenges">Mission challenges</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Galileo_(spacecraft)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Mission challenges">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>Some of the mission challenges that had to be overcome included intense radiation at Jupiter and hardware wear-and-tear, as well as dealing with unexpected technical difficulties.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Radiation-related_anomalies">Radiation-related anomalies</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Galileo_(spacecraft)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Radiation-related anomalies">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Jupiter%27s_Magnetosphere_animation.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1889" data-file-width="2500" decoding="async" height="166" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c3/Jupiter%27s_Magnetosphere_animation.png/220px-Jupiter%27s_Magnetosphere_animation.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c3/Jupiter%27s_Magnetosphere_animation.png/330px-Jupiter%27s_Magnetosphere_animation.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c3/Jupiter%27s_Magnetosphere_animation.png/440px-Jupiter%27s_Magnetosphere_animation.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Jupiter%27s_Magnetosphere_animation.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Jupiter\'s inner magnetosphere and radiation belts</div></div></div>\n<p>Jupiter\'s uniquely harsh radiation environment caused over 20 anomalies over the course of <i>Galileo</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">\'s</span> mission, in addition to the incidents expanded upon below. Despite exceeding its radiation design limit by at least a factor of three, the spacecraft survived all these anomalies. Work-arounds were found eventually for all of these problems, and <i>Galileo</i> was never rendered entirely non-functional by Jupiter\'s radiation. The radiation limits for <i>Galileo</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">\'s</span> computers were based off data returned from <i><a href="/wiki/Pioneer_10" title="Pioneer 10">Pioneers 10</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Pioneer_11" title="Pioneer 11">11</a></i>, since much of the design work was underway before the two <i>Voyagers</i> arrived at Jupiter in 1979.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup>\n</p><p>A typical effect of the radiation was that several of the science instruments suffered increased <a href="/wiki/Signal-to-noise_ratio" title="Signal-to-noise ratio">noise</a> while within about 700,000&#160;km (430,000&#160;mi) of Jupiter. The SSI camera began producing totally white images when the spacecraft was hit by the exceptional <a href="/wiki/Bastille_Day_event" title="Bastille Day event">\'Bastille Day\' coronal mass ejection</a> in 2000, and did so again on subsequent close approaches to Jupiter. The quartz crystal used as the frequency reference for the radio suffered permanent frequency shifts with each Jupiter approach. A spin detector failed, and the spacecraft gyro output was biased by the radiation environment.\n</p><p>The most severe effect of the radiation were current leakages somewhere in the spacecraft\'s power bus, most likely across <a href="/wiki/Brush_(electric)" title="Brush (electric)">brushes</a> at a <a href="/wiki/Bearing_(mechanical)" title="Bearing (mechanical)">spin bearing</a> connecting rotor and stator sections of the orbiter. These current leakages triggered a reset of the onboard computer and caused it to go into safe mode. The resets occurred when the spacecraft was either close to Jupiter or in the region of space magnetically downstream of the Earth. A change to the software was made in April 1999 that allowed the onboard computer to detect these resets and autonomously recover, so as to avoid safe mode.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Main_antenna_problem">Main antenna problem</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Galileo_(spacecraft)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: Main antenna problem">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Galileo_HGA_ribs.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="415" data-file-width="299" decoding="async" height="236" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ce/Galileo_HGA_ribs.svg/170px-Galileo_HGA_ribs.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ce/Galileo_HGA_ribs.svg/255px-Galileo_HGA_ribs.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ce/Galileo_HGA_ribs.svg/340px-Galileo_HGA_ribs.svg.png 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Galileo_HGA_ribs.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Proposed configuration of antenna problem</div></div></div>\n<p><i>Galileo</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">\'s</span> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/High-gain_antenna" title="High-gain antenna">high-gain antenna</a> failed to fully deploy after its first flyby of Earth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-parkes.tracks_48-0"><a href="#cite_note-parkes.tracks-48">[48]</a></sup> The antenna had 18 ribs, like an umbrella and when the driver motor started and put pressure on the ribs, they were supposed to pop out of the cup their tips were held in. Only 15 popped out, leaving the antenna looking like a lop-sided, half-open umbrella. Investigators concluded that during the 4.5 years that <i>Galileo</i> spent in storage after the <a href="/wiki/Space_Shuttle_Challenger_disaster" title="Space Shuttle Challenger disaster">1986 <i>Challenger</i> disaster</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Lubricant" title="Lubricant">lubricants</a> between the tips of the ribs and the cup were eroded and worn away from <a href="/wiki/Vibration" title="Vibration">vibration</a> during three cross-country journeys by truck between California and Florida for the spacecraft.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup> The failed ribs were those closest to the flat-bed trailers carrying <i>Galileo</i> on these trips, which were used instead of air transport to cut costs.  The antenna lubricants were not checked or replaced before launch.\n</p><p>To fix this malfunction, engineers tried thermal-cycling the antenna, rotating the spacecraft up to its maximum spin rate of 10.5&#160;rpm, and "hammering" the antenna deployment motor&#8212;turning it on and off repeatedly&#8212;over 13,000 times, but all attempts failed to open the high-gain antenna.\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Galileo_orbiter_arrival_at_Jupiter_(cropped).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1070" data-file-width="1323" decoding="async" height="178" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/80/Galileo_orbiter_arrival_at_Jupiter_%28cropped%29.jpg/220px-Galileo_orbiter_arrival_at_Jupiter_%28cropped%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/80/Galileo_orbiter_arrival_at_Jupiter_%28cropped%29.jpg/330px-Galileo_orbiter_arrival_at_Jupiter_%28cropped%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/80/Galileo_orbiter_arrival_at_Jupiter_%28cropped%29.jpg/440px-Galileo_orbiter_arrival_at_Jupiter_%28cropped%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Galileo_orbiter_arrival_at_Jupiter_(cropped).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Illustration of <i>Galileo</i> with antenna not fully deployed</div></div></div>\n<p>The associated problem mission managers faced was if one rib popped free, there would be increased pressure on the remaining two, and if one of them popped out the last would be under so much pressure it would never release. The second part of the problem was due to <i>Galileo</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">\'s</span> revised flight plan. The probe had never been intended to approach the Sun any closer than the orbit of Earth, but sending it to Venus would expose it to temperatures at least 50 degrees higher than at Earth distance. So the probe had to be protected from that extra heat, part of which involved adapting some of the computer functions. Forty-one <a href="/wiki/Device_driver" title="Device driver">device drivers</a> had been programmed into the computer, but with no room for any more, the mission planners had to decide which driver they could use in association with the heat protection. They chose the antenna motor reverse driver.\n</p><p>Fortunately, <i>Galileo</i> possessed an additional <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Low-gain_antenna" title="Low-gain antenna">low-gain antenna</a> that was capable of transmitting information back to Earth, although since it transmitted a signal <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Isotropic" title="Isotropic">isotropically</a>, the low-gain antenna\'s <a href="/wiki/Bandwidth_(computing)" title="Bandwidth (computing)">bandwidth</a> was significantly less than what the high-gain antenna\'s would have been; the high-gain antenna was to have transmitted at 134&#160;<a href="/wiki/Kilobit" title="Kilobit">kilobits</a> per second, whereas the low-gain antenna was only intended to transmit at about 8 to 16&#160;bits per second. <i>Galileo</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">\'s</span> low-gain antenna transmitted with a power of about 15 to 20&#160;watts, which, by the time it reached Earth and had been collected by one of the large aperture (70 m) <a href="/wiki/NASA_Deep_Space_Network" title="NASA Deep Space Network">NASA Deep Space Network</a> antennas, had a total power of about &#8722;170&#160;dBm or 10&#160;zeptowatts (10<sup>&#8722;<span style="white-space:nowrap">20</span></sup>&#160;watts).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup> Through the implementation of sophisticated technologies, the arraying of several Deep Space Network antennas and sensitivity upgrades to the receivers used to listen to <i>Galileo</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">\'s</span> signal, data throughput was increased to a maximum of 160&#160;bits per second.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-parkes.tracks_48-1"><a href="#cite_note-parkes.tracks-48">[48]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DeepSpaceNasaDataRate_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-DeepSpaceNasaDataRate-51">[51]</a></sup> By further using data compression, the effective data rate could be raised to 1,000&#160;bits per second.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DeepSpaceNasaDataRate_51-1"><a href="#cite_note-DeepSpaceNasaDataRate-51">[51]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup> The data collected on Jupiter and its moons was stored in the spacecraft\'s onboard <a href="/wiki/Tape_recorder" title="Tape recorder">tape recorder</a>, and transmitted back to Earth during the long <a href="/wiki/Apsis" title="Apsis">apoapsis</a> portion of the probe\'s orbit using the low-gain antenna. At the same time, measurements were made of Jupiter\'s magnetosphere and transmitted back to Earth. The reduction in available bandwidth reduced the total amount of data transmitted throughout the mission, although 70% of <i>Galileo</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">\'s</span> science goals could still be met.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-parkes.tracks_48-2"><a href="#cite_note-parkes.tracks-48">[48]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Tape_recorder_anomalies_and_remote_repair">Tape recorder anomalies and remote repair</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Galileo_(spacecraft)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=34" title="Edit section: Tape recorder anomalies and remote repair">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>The failure of <i>Galileo</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">\'s</span> high-gain antenna meant that data storage to the tape recorder for later compression and playback was absolutely crucial in order to obtain any substantial information from the flybys of Jupiter and its moons. In October 1995, <i>Galileo</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">\'s</span> four-track, 114-<a href="/wiki/Megabyte" title="Megabyte">megabyte</a> digital tape recorder,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup> which was manufactured by <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Odetics_Corporation&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Odetics Corporation (page does not exist)">Odetics Corporation</a>, remained stuck in rewind mode for 15 hours before engineers learned what had happened and sent commands to shut it off. Though the recorder itself was still in working order, the malfunction possibly damaged a length of tape at the end of the reel. This section of tape was subsequently declared "off limits" to any future data recording, and was covered with 25 more turns of tape to secure the section and reduce any further stresses, which could tear it. Because it happened only weeks before <i>Galileo</i> entered orbit around Jupiter, the anomaly prompted engineers to sacrifice data acquisition of almost all of the <a href="/wiki/Io_(moon)" title="Io (moon)">Io</a> and <a href="/wiki/Europa_(moon)" title="Europa (moon)">Europa</a> observations during the orbit insertion phase, in order to focus solely on recording data sent from the Jupiter probe descent.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Parachutes_-_Galileo_Spacecraft_-_GPN-2000-001911_(cropped).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1751" data-file-width="2400" decoding="async" height="161" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c2/Parachutes_-_Galileo_Spacecraft_-_GPN-2000-001911_%28cropped%29.jpg/220px-Parachutes_-_Galileo_Spacecraft_-_GPN-2000-001911_%28cropped%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c2/Parachutes_-_Galileo_Spacecraft_-_GPN-2000-001911_%28cropped%29.jpg/330px-Parachutes_-_Galileo_Spacecraft_-_GPN-2000-001911_%28cropped%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c2/Parachutes_-_Galileo_Spacecraft_-_GPN-2000-001911_%28cropped%29.jpg/440px-Parachutes_-_Galileo_Spacecraft_-_GPN-2000-001911_%28cropped%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Parachutes_-_Galileo_Spacecraft_-_GPN-2000-001911_(cropped).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Wind tunnel test of the atmospheric probe\'s parachute</div></div></div>\n<p>In November 2002, after the completion of the mission\'s only encounter with Jupiter\'s moon <a href="/wiki/Amalthea_(moon)" title="Amalthea (moon)">Amalthea</a>, problems with playback of the tape recorder again plagued <i>Galileo</i>. About 10 minutes after the closest approach of the Amalthea flyby, <i>Galileo</i> stopped collecting data, shut down all of its instruments, and went into <a href="/wiki/Safe_mode_(spacecraft)" title="Safe mode (spacecraft)">safe mode</a>, apparently as a result of exposure to Jupiter\'s intense radiation environment. Though most of the Amalthea data was already written to tape, it was found that the recorder refused to respond to commands telling it to play back data.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup> After weeks of troubleshooting of an identical flight spare of the recorder on the ground, it was determined that the cause of the malfunction was a reduction of light output in three infrared Optek OP133 <a href="/wiki/Light-emitting_diode" title="Light-emitting diode">light-emitting diodes</a> located in the drive electronics of the recorder\'s motor <a href="/wiki/Rotary_encoder" title="Rotary encoder">encoder</a> wheel. The <a href="/wiki/Gallium_arsenide" title="Gallium arsenide">GaAs</a> LEDs had been particularly sensitive to proton-irradiation-induced <a href="/wiki/Crystal" title="Crystal">atomic lattice</a> displacement defects, which greatly decreased their effective light output and caused the drive motor\'s electronics to falsely believe the motor encoder wheel was incorrectly positioned. <i>Galileo</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">\'s</span> flight team then began a series of "<a href="/wiki/Annealing_(metallurgy)" title="Annealing (metallurgy)">annealing</a>" sessions, where current was passed through the LEDs for hours at a time to heat them to a point where some of the crystalline lattice defects would be shifted back into place, thus increasing the LED\'s light output. After about 100 hours of annealing and playback cycles, the recorder was able to operate for up to an hour at a time. After many subsequent playback and cooling cycles, the complete transmission back to Earth of all recorded Amalthea flyby data was successful.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Probe_parachute_deployment">Probe parachute deployment</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Galileo_(spacecraft)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=35" title="Edit section: Probe parachute deployment">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>The atmospheric probe deployed its parachute fifty-three seconds later than anticipated, resulting in a small loss of upper atmospheric readings. This was attributed to wiring problems with an accelerometer that determined when to begin the parachute deployment sequence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="End_of_mission_and_deorbit">End of mission and deorbit</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Galileo_(spacecraft)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=36" title="Edit section: End of mission and deorbit">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Galileo_End.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="432" data-file-width="640" decoding="async" height="149" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/Galileo_End.jpg/220px-Galileo_End.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/Galileo_End.jpg/330px-Galileo_End.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/Galileo_End.jpg/440px-Galileo_End.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Galileo_End.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Illustration of <i>Galileo</i> entering Jupiter\'s atmosphere</div></div></div>\n<p>Two years of Jupiter\'s intense radiation took its toll on the spacecraft\'s systems, and its fuel supply was running low in the early 2000s. <i>Galileo</i> had not been sterilized prior to launch and could have carried bacteria from Earth. Therefore, a plan was formulated to send the probe directly into Jupiter, in an intentional crash to eliminate the possibility of any impact with Jupiter\'s moons and prevent a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Forward_contamination" title="Forward contamination">forward contamination</a>.\n</p><p><i>Galileo</i> flew by <a href="/wiki/Amalthea_(moon)" title="Amalthea (moon)">Amalthea</a> on November 5, 2002,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup> during its 34th orbit, allowing a measurement of the moon\'s mass as it passed within 163&#160;&#177;&#160;11.7&#160;km (101.3&#160;&#177;&#160;7.3&#160;mi) of its surface. On April 14, 2003, <i>Galileo</i> reached its greatest orbital distance from Jupiter for the entire mission since orbital insertion, 26&#160;million&#160;km (16&#160;million&#160;mi), before plunging back towards the gas giant for its final impact.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup> At the completion of its 35th and final circuit around the Jovian system, <i>Galileo</i> impacted the gas giant in darkness just south of the equator on September 21, 2003, at 18:57&#160;UTC. Its impact speed was approximately 173,700&#160;km/h (108,000&#160;mph).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup> The total mission cost was about <span style="white-space: nowrap">US$1.4 billion</span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Follow-on_missions">Follow-on missions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Galileo_(spacecraft)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=37" title="Edit section: Follow-on missions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Europa_Mission_Spacecraft_-_Artist%27s_Rendering.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="7352" data-file-width="8352" decoding="async" height="194" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4d/Europa_Mission_Spacecraft_-_Artist%27s_Rendering.jpg/220px-Europa_Mission_Spacecraft_-_Artist%27s_Rendering.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4d/Europa_Mission_Spacecraft_-_Artist%27s_Rendering.jpg/330px-Europa_Mission_Spacecraft_-_Artist%27s_Rendering.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4d/Europa_Mission_Spacecraft_-_Artist%27s_Rendering.jpg/440px-Europa_Mission_Spacecraft_-_Artist%27s_Rendering.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Europa_Mission_Spacecraft_-_Artist%27s_Rendering.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Concept art of the <i>Europa Clipper</i> spacecraft</div></div></div>\n<p>While <i>Galileo</i> was operating, <i><a href="/wiki/Cassini%E2%80%93Huygens" title="Cassini&#8211;Huygens">Cassini&#8211;Huygens</a></i> coasted by the planet in 2000 en route to Saturn, and it also collected data on Jupiter. <i><a href="/wiki/Ulysses_(spacecraft)" title="Ulysses (spacecraft)">Ulysses</a></i> passed by Jupiter in 1992 and 2004 on its mission to study the Sun\'s polar regions. <i><a href="/wiki/New_Horizons" title="New Horizons">New Horizons</a></i> also passed close by Jupiter in 2007 for a gravity assist en route to Pluto, and it too collected data on the planet. The next mission to orbit Jupiter was the <i><a href="/wiki/Juno_(spacecraft)" title="Juno (spacecraft)">Juno</a></i> spacecraft in July 2016.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Juno"><i>Juno</i></span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Galileo_(spacecraft)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=38" title="Edit section: Juno">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>NASA\'s <i><a href="/wiki/Juno_(spacecraft)" title="Juno (spacecraft)">Juno</a></i> spacecraft, launched in 2011 and planned for a two-year tour of the Jovian system, successfully completed Jupiter orbital insertion on July 4, 2016.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span id="Europa_Orbiter_.28canceled.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Europa_Orbiter_(canceled)">Europa Orbiter (canceled)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Galileo_(spacecraft)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=39" title="Edit section: Europa Orbiter (canceled)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>There was a spare <i>Galileo</i> spacecraft that was considered by the NASA-ESA Outer Planets Study Team in 1983 for a mission to Saturn, but it was passed over in favor of a newer design which became <i><a href="/wiki/Cassini%E2%80%93Huygens" title="Cassini&#8211;Huygens">Cassini&#8211;Huygens</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup> Even before <i>Galileo</i> concluded, NASA considered the <a href="/wiki/Europa_Orbiter" title="Europa Orbiter">Europa Orbiter</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup> which was a mission to Jupiter\'s moon <a href="/wiki/Europa_(moon)" title="Europa (moon)">Europa</a>, but it was canceled in 2002.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Jupiter_Icy_Moons_Explorer">Jupiter Icy Moons Explorer</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Galileo_(spacecraft)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=40" title="Edit section: Jupiter Icy Moons Explorer">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>ESA also is planning to return to the Jovian system with the <a href="/wiki/Jupiter_Icy_Moons_Explorer" title="Jupiter Icy Moons Explorer">Jupiter Icy Moons Explorer</a> (JUICE), which is designed to orbit Ganymede in the 2020s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup> There have been several other attempts at missions that were dedicated to, or included, the Jupiter system as part of their mission plan but did not make it out of the planning stages.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Europa_Multiple_Flyby_Mission">Europa Multiple Flyby Mission</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Galileo_(spacecraft)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=41" title="Edit section: Europa Multiple Flyby Mission">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Following the cancellation of Europa Orbiter, a lower-cost version called Europa Multiple Flyby Mission was studied. This led to the <i><a href="/wiki/Europa_Clipper" title="Europa Clipper">Europa Clipper</a></i> being approved in 2015; it is currently planned for launch in the mid-2020s.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Galileo_team"><i>Galileo</i> team</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Galileo_(spacecraft)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=42" title="Edit section: Galileo team">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:1989_s34_Galileo_Deploy_5.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1000" data-file-width="746" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f9/1989_s34_Galileo_Deploy_5.jpg/255px-1989_s34_Galileo_Deploy_5.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f9/1989_s34_Galileo_Deploy_5.jpg/340px-1989_s34_Galileo_Deploy_5.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a 1989</div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Sts-34-patch.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Sts-34-patch.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Patch for the STS-34 mission</div></div></div>\n<p>Investigators and researchers came from the following institutions, and included famous scientists such as <a href="/wiki/Carl_Sagan" title="Carl Sagan">Carl Sagan</a> and <a href="/wiki/James_Van_Allen" title="James Van Allen">James Van Allen</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ufrgs.galproj_68-0"><a href="#cite_note-ufrgs.galproj-68">[68]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Ames_Research_Center" title="Ames Research Center">Ames Research Center</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Bell_Labs" colspan="2"><div>Payloads are separated by bullets (&#160;<b>&#183;</b> ), launches by pipes ( | ). Manned flights are indicated in <b>bold text</b>. Uncatalogued launch failures are listed in <i>italics</i>. Payloads deployed from other spacecraft are denoted in (brackets).</div></td></tr></tbody></table></div>\n<div aria-labelledby="Galileo_Galilei" class="navbox" role="navigation"
